<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Arctic</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Polar region of the Earth's northern hemisphere</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Arctic_(disambiguation)" title="Arctic (disambiguation)">Arctic (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_(orthographic_projection).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="541" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Arctic_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/330px-Arctic_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Arctic_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/495px-Arctic_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Arctic_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg/660px-Arctic_%28orthographic_projection%29.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_(orthographic_projection).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>, currently at roughly 66° north of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>, defines the boundary of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_seas" title="Arctic seas">arctic seas</a> and lands</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3639" data-file-width="4770" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf/page1-220px-Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf/page1-330px-Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf/page1-440px-Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Political_Map_of_the_Arctic.pdf" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A political map showing land ownership within the Arctic region</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctica_surface.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="941" data-file-width="978" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Arctica_surface.jpg/220px-Arctica_surface.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Arctica_surface.jpg/330px-Arctica_surface.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Arctica_surface.jpg/440px-Arctica_surface.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctica_surface.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artificially coloured topographical map of the Arctic region</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg/220px-Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg/330px-Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg/440px-Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunny_Skies_over_the_Arctic_in_Late_June_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Moderate_Resolution_Imaging_Spectroradiometer" title="Moderate Resolution Imaging Spectroradiometer">MODIS</a> image of the Arctic</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Arctic</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahd_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-ahd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[Note 1]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth" title="Polar regions of Earth">polar region</a> located at the northernmost part of Earth. The Arctic consists of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>, adjacent seas, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish_Realm" title="Danish Realm">Denmark</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>), <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Murmansk_Oblast" title="Murmansk Oblast">Murmansk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nenets_Autonomous_Okrug" title="Nenets Autonomous Okrug">Nenets Okrug</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novaya_Zemlya" title="Novaya Zemlya">Novaya Zemlya</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (<a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>). Land within the Arctic region has seasonally varying snow and <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">ice cover</a>, with predominantly treeless <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> (permanently frozen underground ice) containing <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>. Arctic seas contain seasonal <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> in many places.
</p><p>The Arctic region is a unique area among Earth's ecosystems. The cultures in the region and the Arctic <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a> have adapted to its cold and extreme conditions. Life in the Arctic includes <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a>, land animals, plants and human societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> Arctic land is bordered by the <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">subarctic</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_etymology">Definition and etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition and etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word Arctic comes from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> word <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CF%81%CE%BA%CF%84%CE%B9%CE%BA%CF%8C%CF%82#Greek" title="wikt:ἀρκτικός">ἀρκτικός</a></span></span> (<i>arktikos</i>), "near the Bear, northern"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> and from the word <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CF%81%CE%BA%CF%84%CE%BF%CF%82#Greek" title="wikt:ἄρκτος">ἄρκτος</a></span></span> (<i>arktos</i>), meaning <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bear</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> The name refers either to the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Major" title="Ursa Major">Ursa Major</a>, the "Great Bear", which is prominent in the northern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">celestial sphere</a>, or to the constellation <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Minor" title="Ursa Minor">Ursa Minor</a>, the "Little Bear", which contains the celestial north pole (currently very near <a href="/wiki/Polaris" title="Polaris">Polaris</a>, the current north Pole Star, or North Star).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a number of definitions of what area is contained within the Arctic. The area can be defined as north of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a> (about 66° 34'N), the approximate southern limit of the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_sun" title="Midnight sun">midnight sun</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Polar_night" title="Polar night">polar night</a>. Another definition of the Arctic, which is popular with <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecologists</a>, is the region in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> where the average temperature for the warmest month (July) is below 10 °C (50 °F); the northernmost <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a> roughly follows the <a href="/wiki/Contour_line#Temperature_and_related_subjects" title="Contour line">isotherm</a> at the boundary of this region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_Arctic" title="Climate of the Arctic">Climate of the Arctic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming_in_the_Arctic" title="Global warming in the Arctic">Global warming in the Arctic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Laponie001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Laponie001.jpg/220px-Laponie001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Laponie001.jpg/330px-Laponie001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Laponie001.jpg/440px-Laponie001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Laponie001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A snowy landscape of <a href="/wiki/Inari,_Finland" title="Inari, Finland">Inari</a> located in <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Lapland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The Arctic is characterized by cold winters and cool summers. Its precipitation mostly comes in the form of snow and is low, with most of the area receiving less than 50 cm (20 in). High winds often stir up snow, creating the illusion of continuous snowfall. Average winter temperatures can go as low as −40 °C (−40 °F), and the coldest recorded temperature is approximately −68 °C (−90 °F). Coastal Arctic climates are moderated by oceanic influences, having generally warmer temperatures and heavier snowfalls than the colder and drier interior areas. The Arctic is affected by current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, leading to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">Arctic sea ice shrinkage</a>, diminished ice in the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_methane_release" title="Arctic methane release">Arctic methane release</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> thaws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> The melting of Greenland's ice sheet is linked to polar amplification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the poleward migration of the planet's isotherms (about 56 km (35 mi) per decade during the past 30 years as a consequence of global warming), the Arctic region (as defined by <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a> and temperature) is currently shrinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Perhaps the most alarming result of this is Arctic sea ice shrinkage. There is a large variance in predictions of Arctic sea ice loss, with models showing near-complete to complete loss in September from 2035 to some time around 2067.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Flora and fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Arctic life is characterized by adaptation to short growing seasons with long periods of sunlight, and cold, dark, snow-covered winter conditions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3484" data-file-width="5226" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg/220px-Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg/330px-Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg/440px-Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_poppy_among_rocks.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Papaver_radicatum" title="Papaver radicatum">Arctic poppy</a> in bloom within the <a href="/wiki/Qausuittuq_National_Park" title="Qausuittuq National Park">Qausuittuq National Park</a> on <a href="/wiki/Bathurst_Island_(Nunavut)" title="Bathurst Island (Nunavut)">Bathurst Island</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arctic_vegetation" title="Arctic vegetation">Arctic vegetation</a> is composed of plants such as <a href="/wiki/Subshrub" title="Subshrub">dwarf shrubs</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graminoids" title="Graminoids">graminoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herb" title="Herb">herbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>, which all grow relatively close to the ground, forming <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>. An example of a dwarf shrub is the <a href="/wiki/Bearberry" title="Bearberry">bearberry</a>. As one moves northward, the amount of warmth available for plant growth decreases considerably. In the northernmost areas, plants are at their metabolic limits, and small differences in the total amount of summer warmth make large differences in the amount of energy available for maintenance, growth and reproduction. Colder summer temperatures cause the size, abundance, productivity and variety of plants to decrease. Trees cannot grow in the Arctic, but in its warmest parts, shrubs are common and can reach 2 m (6 ft 7 in) in height; <a href="/wiki/Cyperaceae" title="Cyperaceae">sedges</a>, mosses and lichens can form thick layers. In the coldest parts of the Arctic, much of the ground is bare; <a href="/wiki/Non-vascular_plant" title="Non-vascular plant">non-vascular plants</a> such as lichens and mosses predominate, along with a few scattered grasses and <a href="/wiki/Forb" title="Forb">forbs</a> (like the <a href="/wiki/Papaver_radicatum" title="Papaver radicatum">Arctic poppy</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Arctic_cetaceans" title="List of Arctic cetaceans">List of Arctic cetaceans</a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref" role="note">See also the categories <a href="/wiki/Category:Fauna_of_the_Arctic" title="Category:Fauna of the Arctic">Fauna of the Arctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Mammals_of_the_Arctic" title="Category:Mammals of the Arctic">Mammals of the Arctic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muskus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1064" data-file-width="1488" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Muskus.jpg/220px-Muskus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Muskus.jpg/330px-Muskus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Muskus.jpg/440px-Muskus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muskus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muskox" title="Muskox">Muskox</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg/170px-Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg/255px-Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg/340px-Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Snowy_Owl_Portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a></div></div></div>
<p>Herbivores on the tundra include the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_hare" title="Arctic hare">Arctic hare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lemming" title="Lemming">lemming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muskox" title="Muskox">muskox</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">caribou</a>. They are preyed on by the <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bear" title="Grizzly bear">Grizzly bear</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_wolf" title="Arctic wolf">Arctic wolf</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bear</a> is also a predator, though it prefers to hunt for marine life from the ice. There are also many <a href="/wiki/Category:Birds_of_the_Arctic" title="Category:Birds of the Arctic">birds</a> and marine species endemic to the colder regions. Other terrestrial animals include <a href="/wiki/Wolverine" title="Wolverine">wolverines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">moose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dall_sheep" title="Dall sheep">Dall sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoat" title="Stoat">ermines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_ground_squirrel" title="Arctic ground squirrel">Arctic ground squirrels</a>. Marine mammals include <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a>, and several species of <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetacean</a>—<a href="/wiki/Baleen_whale" title="Baleen whale">baleen whales</a> and also <a href="/wiki/Narwhal" title="Narwhal">narwhals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orca" title="Orca">orcas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Beluga_whale" title="Beluga whale">belugas</a>. An excellent and famous example of a <a href="/wiki/Ring_species" title="Ring species">ring species</a> exists and has been described around the Arctic Circle in the form of the <i><a href="/wiki/Larus" title="Larus">Larus</a></i> gulls.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_resources">Natural resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Natural resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Natural_resources_of_the_Arctic" title="Natural resources of the Arctic">Natural resources of the Arctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_exploration_in_the_Arctic" title="Petroleum exploration in the Arctic">Petroleum exploration in the Arctic</a></div>
<p>The Arctic includes copious <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> (oil, gas, minerals, fresh water, fish and, if the subarctic is included, forest) to which modern technology and the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_resources_race" title="Arctic resources race">economic opening up of Russia</a> have given significant new opportunities. The interest of the tourism industry is also on the increase.
</p><p>The Arctic contains some of the last and most extensive continuous <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a> areas in the world, and its significance in preserving <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotypes</a> is considerable. The increasing presence of humans fragments vital habitats. The Arctic is particularly susceptible to the abrasion of <a href="/wiki/Groundcover" title="Groundcover">groundcover</a> and to the disturbance of the rare breeding grounds of the animals that are characteristic to the region. The Arctic also holds 1/5 of the Earth's water supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleontology">Paleontology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Paleontology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_fossils.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2024" data-file-width="2880" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Arctic_fossils.jpg/220px-Arctic_fossils.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Arctic_fossils.jpg/330px-Arctic_fossils.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Arctic_fossils.jpg/440px-Arctic_fossils.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_fossils.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossils" title="Fossils">fossils</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Arctic" title="Canadian Arctic">Canadian Arctic</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous time period</a>, the Arctic still had seasonal snows, though only a light dusting and not enough to permanently hinder plant growth. Animals such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Chasmosaurus" title="Chasmosaurus">Chasmosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hypacrosaurus" title="Hypacrosaurus">Hypacrosaurus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Troodon" title="Troodon">Troodon</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Edmontosaurus" title="Edmontosaurus">Edmontosaurus</a></i> may have all migrated north to take advantage of the summer growing season, and migrated south to warmer climes when winter came. A similar situation may also have been found amongst <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> that lived in <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a> regions, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Muttaburrasaurus" title="Muttaburrasaurus">Muttaburrasaurus</a></i> of Australia.
</p><p>However, others claim that dinosaurs lived year-round at very high latitudes, such as near the <a href="/wiki/Colville_River_(Alaska)" title="Colville River (Alaska)">Colville River</a>, which is now at about 70° N but at the time (70 million years ago) was 10° further north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_population">Indigenous population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Indigenous population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_peoples" title="Circumpolar peoples">Circumpolar peoples</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Siberia" title="Indigenous peoples of Siberia">Indigenous peoples of Siberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inuit_Circumpolar_Council" title="Inuit Circumpolar Council">Inuit Circumpolar Council</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="942" data-file-width="881" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png/260px-Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png/390px-Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png/520px-Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Circumpolar_coastal_human_population_distribution_ca._2009.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Circumpolar coastal human population distribution c. 2009 (includes indigenous and non-indigenous).</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest inhabitants of North America's central and eastern Arctic are referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_small_tool_tradition" title="Arctic small tool tradition">Arctic small tool tradition</a> (AST) and existed c. 2500 BCE. AST consisted of several <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Eskimo" title="Paleo-Eskimo">Paleo-Eskimo</a> cultures, including the <a href="/wiki/Independence_I_culture" title="Independence I culture">Independence cultures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-Dorset" title="Pre-Dorset">Pre-Dorset</a> culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbon_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbon-18">[17]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dorset_culture" title="Dorset culture">Dorset culture</a> (<a href="/wiki/Inuktitut" title="Inuktitut">Inuktitut</a>: <i>Tuniit</i> or <i>Tunit</i>) refers to the next inhabitants of central and eastern Arctic. The Dorset culture evolved because of technological and economic changes during the period of 1050–550 BCE. With the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>/<a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a> peninsula, the Dorset culture vanished around 1500 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Supported by genetic testing, evidence shows that descendants of the Dorset culture, known as the <a href="/wiki/Sadlermiut" title="Sadlermiut">Sadlermiut</a>, survived in Aivilik, <a href="/wiki/Southampton_Island" title="Southampton Island">Southampton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coats_Island" title="Coats Island">Coats Islands</a>, until the beginning of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Dorset/<a href="/wiki/Thule_people" title="Thule people">Thule culture</a> transition dates around the ninth–10th centuries CE. Scientists theorize that there may have been cross-contact of the two cultures with sharing of technology, such as fashioning harpoon heads, or the Thule may have found Dorset remnants and adapted their ways with the predecessor culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Others believe the Thule displaced the Dorset.
</p><p>By 1300 CE, the <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a>, present-day Arctic inhabitants and descendants of Thule culture, had settled in west Greenland, and moved into east Greenland over the following century (<a href="/wiki/Inughuit" title="Inughuit">Inughuit</a><b>,</b> <a href="/wiki/Kalaallit" title="Kalaallit">Kalaallit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunumiit" title="Tunumiit">Tunumiit</a> are modern Greenlandic Inuit groups descended from Thule). Over time, the Inuit have migrated throughout the Arctic regions of Eastern Russia, the United States, Canada, and Greenland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other Circumpolar North indigenous peoples include the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_people" title="Chukchi people">Chukchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evenks" title="Evenks">Evenks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inupiat_people" title="Inupiat people">Iñupiat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khanty_people" title="Khanty people">Khanty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koryaks" title="Koryaks">Koryaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nenets_people" title="Nenets people">Nenets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">Sami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yukaghir_people" title="Yukaghir people">Yukaghir</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gwich%27in" title="Gwich'in">Gwich'in</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yupik_peoples" title="Yupik peoples">Yupik</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_cooperation_and_politics">International cooperation and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: International cooperation and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arctic_cooperation_and_politics" title="Arctic cooperation and politics">Arctic cooperation and politics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1575" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/220px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/330px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg/440px-Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polar_bears_near_north_pole.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">Polar bears</a> on the sea ice of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>, near the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>. <a href="/wiki/USS_Honolulu_(SSN-718)" title="USS Honolulu (SSN-718)">USS <i>Honolulu</i></a> pictured.</div></div></div>
<p>The eight Arctic nations (Canada, Kingdom of Denmark [Greenland &amp; The Faroe Islands], Finland, Iceland, Norway, Sweden, Russia, and USA) are all members of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Council" title="Arctic Council">Arctic Council</a>, as are organizations representing six indigenous populations. The council operates on consensus basis, mostly dealing with environmental treaties and not addressing boundary or resource disputes.
</p><p>Though <a href="/wiki/Arctic_cooperation_and_politics" title="Arctic cooperation and politics">Arctic policy priorities differ</a>, every Arctic nation is concerned about sovereignty/defense, resource development, shipping routes, and environmental protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buixade_Farre_2014_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buixade_Farre_2014-23">[22]</a></sup> Much work remains on regulatory agreements regarding shipping, tourism, and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_resources_race" title="Arctic resources race">resource development</a> in Arctic waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Arctic shipping is subject to some regulatory control through the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_for_Ships_Operating_in_Polar_Waters" title="International Code for Ships Operating in Polar Waters">International Code for Ships Operating in Polar Waters</a>, adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Maritime_Organization" title="International Maritime Organization">International Maritime Organization</a> on 1 January 2017 and applies to all ships in Arctic waters over 500 tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMOPolar_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMOPolar-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMOPolar1Year_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMOPolar1Year-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research in the Arctic has long been a collaborative international effort, evidenced by the <a href="/wiki/International_Polar_Year" title="International Polar Year">International Polar Year</a>. The <a href="/wiki/International_Arctic_Science_Committee" title="International Arctic Science Committee">International Arctic Science Committee</a>, hundreds of scientists and specialists of the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Council" title="Arctic Council">Arctic Council</a>, and the Barents Euro-Arctic Council are more examples of collaborative international Arctic research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_claims">Territorial claims</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Territorial claims">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_the_Arctic" title="Territorial claims in the Arctic">Territorial claims in the Arctic</a></div>
<p>No country owns the geographic <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> or the region of the Arctic Ocean surrounding it. The surrounding six Arctic states that border the Arctic Ocean—Canada, Kingdom of Denmark (with Greenland), Iceland, Norway, Russia, and the United States—are limited to a 200 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical miles</a> (370 km; 230 mi) exclusive economic zone (EEZ) off their coasts. Two Arctic states (Finland and Sweden) do not have direct access to the Arctic Ocean.
</p><p>Upon ratification of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea</a>, a country has ten years to make claims to an extended continental shelf beyond its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exclusive_Economic_Zone" title="Exclusive Economic Zone">200 nautical mile zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buixade_Farre_2014_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buixade_Farre_2014-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> Due to this, Norway (which ratified the convention in 1996),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ratif-29">[28]</a></sup> Russia (ratified in 1997),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ratif-29">[28]</a></sup> Canada (ratified in 2003)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-ratif-29">[28]</a></sup> and the Kingdom of Denmark (ratified in 2004)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-ratif-29">[28]</a></sup> launched projects to establish claims that certain sectors of the Arctic seabed should belong to their territories.
</p><p>On 2 August 2007, two Russian <a href="/wiki/Bathyscaphe" title="Bathyscaphe">bathyscaphes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIR_(submersible)" title="MIR (submersible)">MIR-1 and MIR-2</a>, for the first time in history descended to the Arctic <a href="/wiki/Seabed" title="Seabed">seabed</a> beneath the North Pole and placed there a <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Russia" title="Flag of Russia">Russian flag</a> made of rust-proof <a href="/wiki/Titanium_alloy" title="Titanium alloy">titanium alloy</a>. The flag-placing during <a href="/wiki/Arktika_2007" title="Arktika 2007">Arktika 2007</a> generated commentary on and concern for a race for control of the Arctic's vast hydrocarbon resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage,_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage,_and_bathymetry.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="952" data-file-width="1003" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png/220px-Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png/330px-Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png/440px-Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage%2C_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage%2C_and_bathymetry.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Arctic_region_showing_the_Northeast_Passage,_the_Northern_Sea_Route_and_Northwest_Passage,_and_bathymetry.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Arctic region showing the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Passage" title="Northeast Passage">Northeast Passage</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sea_Route" title="Northern Sea Route">Northern Sea Route</a> within it, and the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Foreign ministers and other officials representing Canada, the Kingdom of Denmark, Norway, Russia, and the United States met in <a href="/wiki/Ilulissat" title="Ilulissat">Ilulissat, Greenland</a> on 28 May 2008 at the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean_Conference" title="Arctic Ocean Conference">Arctic Ocean Conference</a> and announced the <a href="/wiki/Ilulissat_Declaration" title="Ilulissat Declaration">Ilulissat Declaration</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> blocking any "new comprehensive international legal regime to govern the Arctic Ocean," and pledging "the orderly settlement of any possible overlapping claims."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buixade_Farre_2014_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Buixade_Farre_2014-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2012, the Kingdom of Denmark is claiming the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a> based on the Lomonosov Ridge between Greenland and over the North Pole to the northern limit of the Russian EEZ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russian Federation is also claiming a large swath of seabed along the <a href="/wiki/Lomonosov_Ridge" title="Lomonosov Ridge">Lomonosov Ridge</a> but, unlike Denmark, confined its claim to its side of the Arctic. In August 2015, Russia made a supplementary submission for the expansion of the external borders of its continental shelf in the Arctic Ocean, asserting that the eastern part of the Lomonosov Ridge and the Mendeleyev Ridge are an extension of the Eurasian continent. In August 2016, the UN Commission on the Limits of the Continental Shelf began to consider Russia's submission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Canada claims the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a> as part of its <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Internal_Waters" title="Canadian Internal Waters">internal waters</a> belonging to Canada, while the United States and most maritime nations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> regards it as an <a href="/wiki/Territorial_waters" title="Territorial waters">international strait</a>, which means that foreign vessels have right of transit passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arctic_exploration" title="Arctic exploration">Arctic exploration</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_exploration_in_the_Arctic" title="Petroleum exploration in the Arctic">Petroleum exploration in the Arctic</a></div>
<p>Since 1937, the larger portion of the Asian-side Arctic region has been extensively explored by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Soviet_and_Russian_crewed_drifting_ice_stations&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Soviet and Russian crewed drifting ice stations (page does not exist)">Soviet and Russian crewed drifting ice stations</a>. Between 1937 and 1991, 88 international polar crews established and occupied scientific settlements on the <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">drift ice</a> and were carried thousands of kilometres by the ice flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pollution_in_the_Arctic_Ocean" title="Pollution in the Arctic Ocean">Pollution in the Arctic Ocean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arctic_haze" title="Arctic haze">Arctic haze</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Contamination_pathways_large.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="669" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Contamination_pathways_large.jpg/220px-Contamination_pathways_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Contamination_pathways_large.jpg/330px-Contamination_pathways_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Contamination_pathways_large.jpg/440px-Contamination_pathways_large.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Contamination_pathways_large.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Long-range pollution pathways to the Arctic</div></div></div>
<p>The Arctic is comparatively clean, although there are certain ecologically difficult localized <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> problems that present a serious threat to people's health living around these pollution sources. Due to the prevailing worldwide sea and air currents, the Arctic area is the fallout region for long-range transport <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a>, and in some places the concentrations exceed the levels of densely populated urban areas. An example of this is the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Arctic_haze" title="Arctic haze">Arctic haze</a>, which is commonly blamed on long-range pollutants. Another example is with the <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a> of PCB's (<a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">polychlorinated biphenyls</a>) in Arctic wildlife and people.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preservation">Preservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Preservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Save_the_Arctic" title="Save the Arctic">Save the Arctic</a></div>
<p>There have been many proposals to preserve the Arctic over the years. Most recently a group of stars at the Rio Earth Summit, on 21 June 2012, proposed protecting the Arctic, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Treaty_System" title="Antarctic Treaty System">Antarctic protection</a>. The initial focus of the campaign will be a UN resolution creating a global sanctuary around the pole, and a ban on oil drilling and unsustainable fishing in the Arctic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Arctic has climate change rates that are amongst the highest in the world. Due to the major impacts to the region from climate change the near climate future of the region will be extremely different under all scenarios of warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_warming">Global warming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Global warming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming_in_the_Arctic" title="Global warming in the Arctic">Global warming in the Arctic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/220px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/330px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/440px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_shrinkage" title="Arctic shrinkage">Arctic sea ice coverage</a> as of 2007 compared to 2005 and compared to 1979–2000 average</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects of global warming</a> in the Arctic include rising temperatures, loss of <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a>, and melting of the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_ice_sheet" title="Greenland ice sheet">Greenland ice sheet</a>. Potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_methane_release" title="Arctic methane release">methane release</a> from the region, especially through the thawing of <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methane_clathrates" title="Methane clathrates">methane clathrates</a>, is also a concern. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Polar_amplification" title="Polar amplification">amplified response of the Arctic</a> to global warming, it is often seen as a leading indicator of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. The melting of Greenland's ice sheet is linked to polar amplification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Arctic is especially vulnerable to the effects of any <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, as has become apparent with the reduction of sea ice in recent years. <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">Climate models</a> predict much greater warming in the Arctic than the global average,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HassolArctic_Climate_Impact_Assessment2004_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-HassolArctic_Climate_Impact_Assessment2004-43">[42]</a></sup> resulting in significant international attention to the region. In particular, there are concerns that Arctic shrinkage, a consequence of melting glaciers and other ice in Greenland, could soon contribute to a substantial rise in sea levels worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current Arctic warming is leading to ancient carbon being released from thawing <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a>, leading to <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> production by micro-organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> Release of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> stored in permafrost could cause abrupt and severe global warming,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> as they are potent <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climate change is also predicted to have a large impact on tundra vegetation, causing an increase of shrubs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> and having a negative impact on bryophytes and lichens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from concerns regarding the detrimental effects of warming in the Arctic, some potential opportunities have gained attention. The melting of the ice is making the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>, the shipping routes through the northernmost latitudes, more navigable, raising the possibility that the Arctic region will become a prime <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade route</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> One harbinger of the opening navigability of the Arctic took place in the summer of 2016 when the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_Serenity" title="Crystal Serenity">Crystal Serenity</a> successfully navigated the Northwest Passage, a first for a large cruise ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_(15226568739).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2146" data-file-width="3815" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_%2815226568739%29.jpg/220px-Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_%2815226568739%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_%2815226568739%29.jpg/330px-Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_%2815226568739%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_%2815226568739%29.jpg/440px-Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_%2815226568739%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Svolv%C3%A6r_Harbor_(15226568739).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The shrinking Arctic: Parts of Norway inside the Arctic Circle has a temperate climate with the 1991-2020 normals, such as <a href="/wiki/Skrova" title="Skrova">Skrova</a> near <a href="/wiki/Svolv%C3%A6r" title="Svolvær">Svolvær</a> with mean annual temperature of 6 °C (43 °F), four months above 10°C and no month below 0 °C (32 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In addition, it is believed that the Arctic seabed may contain substantial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a> which may become accessible if the ice covering them melts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> These factors have led to recent international debates as to which nations can claim sovereignty or ownership over the waters of the Arctic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada_sovereignty_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada_sovereignty-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic_waters">Arctic waters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Arctic waters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 22em;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arctic_lands">Arctic lands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Arctic lands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arctic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The word was originally pronounced without the <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/k/</span> sound, but the pronunciation with the k sound is nowadays very common. The "c" was added to the spelling for etymological reasons<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahd_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-ahd-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and then began to be pronounced, but (as with other <a href="/wiki/Spelling_pronunciation" title="Spelling pronunciation">spelling pronunciations</a>) at first only by less-educated people.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
